Legion of the Slavic Nations
KPRP
Komunistyczna Partia Robotnicza Polski (Polish Communist Workers' Party)\n§LFounded in the wake of the Russian Revolution by Polish communists, the KPRP's heyday was short. After the collapse of Soviet Russia and the successes of the Western syndicalist revolutions, the KPRP is a shell of a movement, forced underground and fighting for relevance even within the far left.§!\n
ZZZ
Związek Związków Zawodowych (Union of Trade Unions)\n§LThe core of the Revolutionary Committee, the Union of Trade Unions is made up of anarchist and syndicalist exiles and emigrés in France, organising underground activities in Poland proper. The ZZZ is an effort to unite the different syndicalist-minded groups under Chairman Kazimierz Zakrzewski's leadership. The main part of the ZZZ is composed of the Piłsudskian Generalna Federacja Pracy (The General Federation of Labour), and Lew Marek's anarcho-syndicalist and internationalist Anarchistyczna Federacja Polski (Anarchist Federation of Poland).§!\n
Centrolew - PPS-L
PPS-L
Polska Partia Socjalistyczna - Lewica (Polish Socialist Party - Left)\n§LThe PPS's radical wing, led by the anarchist Zygmunt Zaremba, is a minority within the party. They act as a liaison between the Revolutionary Committee and the moderate PPS, organising underground activities under the PPS's wing.§!\n
Centrolew - PPS
PPS
Polska Partia Socjalistyczna (Polish Socialist Party)\n§LThe social-democratic PPS is one of the two major opposition parties, forming the Centrolew bloc alongside the People's Party. Norbert Barlicki's party has strong sympathies with both republican and socialist anti-monarchist movements, but currently maintains a carefully pragmatic stance towards the Kingdom.§!\n
Polska Partia Socjalistyczna (Polish Socialist Party)\n§LThe social-democratic PPS and their allies managed to overthrow the kingdom and promise a new, more democratic Poland. They seek further parliamentarism, building a strong social safety network and restricting the power of Poland's aristocracy and business elite.§!\n
Centrolew - PSL
PSL
Polskie Stronnictwo Ludowe (Polish People's Party)\n§LThe second half of Centrolew is the People's Party, the result of the unification of several populist, liberal, and agrarian parties. Having strong ties with its equivalent in Galicia, Maksymilian Malinowski's party serves as the main vehicle for republicanism, even if it currently maintains a pragmatic stance towards the monarchy.§!\n
Polskie Stronnictwo Ludowe (Polish People's Party)\n§LOriginally formed out of several populist, liberal, and agrarian parties, the People's Party have successfully achieved power after the Clover Revolution, and is now in a position to build the Poland they have promised to build: where peasant, priest and lord are all equal before the law.§!\n
MKP
Międzypartyjne Koło Polityczne (Interpartisan Political Circle)\n§LFormed in 1915 out of moderate National Democrat and liberal parties, the MKP decided to collaborate with the Central Powers. It is a coalition of the Democratic-National Party, the Party of Real Politics, the Polish Progressive Party, the Party of Polish Statehood and a number of other organisations. The party acts as a representative of the middle ground in Polish politics. It would be considered socially conservative, economically liberal and constitutionalist, and is supported by the middle class and economic sector in particular.§!\n
ChZJN
Chrześcijański Związek Jedności Narodowej (Christian Union of National Unity)\n§LAn alliance of Christian Democratic and National Democratic parties which disagree with MKP's economically liberal approach and pro-German foreign policy. ChZJN, or Chjena, champions for national democracy and Catholic social doctrine, and while pragmatic towards the monarchy, seeks greater independence for Poland through legal means.§!\n
Chrześcijański Związek Jedności Narodowej (Christian Union of National Unity)\n§LAn alliance of Christian Democratic and National Democratic parties, the deeply Catholic and nationalist ChZJN seeks to safeguard Poland's traditional social hierarchy, independence and the Church's position as the soul of the nation.§!\n
MKP-SP
Międzypartyjne Koło Polityczne - Stronnictwo Państwowe (Statehood Party)\n§LThe conservative and openly pro-German wing of the circle. Led by Władysław Studnicki, the party seeks closer relations with Berlin and to uphold the traditional rights of the szlachta.§!\n
Geheimrat
Geheimrat (Privy Council)\n§LThe Geheimrat is an informal advisory board to King August IV, composed mostly of the King's German and Austrian military and economy advisors and pro-German Polish magnates. The Geheimrat has no interest in Polish liberties, but rather seeks to maintain the King's position and Austro-German interests in Poland. The unofficial head of the Privy Council is the most influential German citizen in the country, leader of the military mission, Wolfgang von Kries.§!\n
Generalgouvernement Warschau
Generalgouvernement Warschau\n§LThe General Government of Warsaw is the final nail in the coffin of Poland's independence. Supported by the loyalist generals and Germans, the Governor-General rules with an iron fist, mercilessly quelling any dissent. Elections and the constitution are suspended indefinitely.§!\n
LN
Liga Narodowa (National League)\n§LEstablished in 1893, the National League began as a conspiratorial organisation created from the Endecja movement. The radicals of the Endecja under Roman Dmowski are not welcome in Poland, and their leadership is exiled in Moscow. During the years, they have forged connections within both the Russian far-right and Polish underground organisations, plotting to liberate Poland one day from external and internal enemies.§!\n
ZSP
Związek Syndykalistów Polskich (Union of Polish Syndicalists)\n§LFounded by the supporters of Kazimierz Zakrzewski, the ZSP is officially a broadly syndicalist and democratic organisation, but in practice adheres to Zakrzewski's personal ideology. The mass movement is largely based around Zakrzewski's role as the leader of the revolution, and is made immensely popular because of the patriotism of the common people, and their mistrust towards \

SZW
Syndykalistyczny Związek \
PPS
Polska Partia Socjalistyczna (Polish Socialist Party)\n§LThe former radical wing of the PPS has found itself as the moderates of the socialist republic. They seek a democratic and relatively decentralised Poland, fearing that the revolution will yet turn on itself and become tyrannical, like so many revolutions before it.§!\n
PPS-P
Polska Partia Socjalistyczna - Prawica (Polish Socialist Party - Right)\n§LNow in the minority, the right-wing faction of the PPS is lukewarm at best towards the revolution, and is best described as fellow-travellers seeking to maintain at least some aspects of parliamentarism in Poland.§!\n
PC
Polskie Centrum (Polish Centre)\n§LThe Centre is a minor conservative-liberal party that is mostly composed of earlier monarchists and the moderates of the MKP who did not retire from politics. They prefer keeping economic ties to Germany and Austria would oppose any war with either, seeing it as the utmost folly.§!\n
SPZ
Stronnictwo Państwowo-Zachowawcze (State Preservation Party)\n§LThe State Preservation Party is a conservative grouping of the ex-MKP politicians who speak for keeping many of the structures from the kingdom and avoiding dramatic reforms or foreign policy changes, as they see those as possibly harmful for Poland's security.§!\n
OZN
Obóz Zjednoczenia Narodowego (Camp of National Unity)\n§LThe OZN is essentially a political arm of Edward Rydz-Śmigły's military clique. It presents itself as an apolitical organisation that seeks to unite all political groupings in Poland under one banner. In practice, its political programme is almost copied from the National Democracy movement, mostly composed of militarism, nationalism and anti-Semitism.§!\n
SN
Stronnictwo Narodowe (National Party)\n§LAfter the National Democrat movement was allowed to organise openly again, the disciples of Roman Dmowski founded the National Party as their political arm. While on paper the movement is merely Christian conservative, in practice the party is very radical. As most of the moderate National Democrats are involved in ChZJN, the National Party is filled with those who were banned earlier - far-right radicals around Adam Doboszyński and hardcore Endeks with no love for the democratic system.§!\n
ON
Organizacja Narodowa (National Organisation)\n§LIn Adam Doboszyński's theory of the nation, the multiparty system is anathema to national unity. Instead, the elite should be united under a single political organisation, which is able to coordinate the entirety of society according to Christian and distributionist doctrines. This group of virtuous men will act as the teachers and organisers for the people, and ensure that the political hygiene of the masses is developed.§!\n

August IV
§YBorn:§! §LJanuary 29th, 1887, in the Potsdam City Palace, Potsdam, Province of Brandenburg, Kingdom of Prussia, German Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBorn August Wilhelm Heinrich Günther Viktor of Prussia, August IV of the house of Hohenzollern was crowned King of Poland in the silver regalia of August III in 1921. As a Prince in Germany, he was criticised by his father, Kaiser Wilhelm II, for his love of jewellery and for keeping the company of artists in his Potsdam villa. As King of Poland for the last fifteen years, he has attempted to rule well, despite his reign being dogged by allegations of his own effeminate personal mannerisms, as well as by his strained relationship with his wife, neither of which serve him well in a deeply conservative, Catholic country.\n\nOn a political level, he has been compelled to hand more power to the Sejm, becoming a genuine constitutional monarch whose functions are largely ceremonial, and whose prerogative powers are largely wielded by his prime minister. King August's unfortunate fate is seemingly to be disliked by all sides: to the nationalists he is a symbol of intolerable German domination, to the pragmatists he is a necessary evil, and to the Kaiser he is his least favourite son.§!
Aleksander II
Alexander Ferdinand von Hohenzollern
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 26th, 1912, in Berlin, Province of Brandenburg, Kingdom of Prussia, German Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LPrince Alexander Ferdinand was only eight years old when his family moved to Poland. His youth would be overshadowed by the quite distant and difficult relationship of his parents, and the pressure of expectations: he would have to take up the throne of Poland eventually. Alexander pursued a career in the military, following typical footsteps for a German aristocrat. While he has been educated in Polish culture and language, he remains mediocre in it at best. That does not stop him from being the most popular member of the Polish royal house, and it is widely hoped that he will eventually marry a Polish woman.§!
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 26th, 1912, in Berlin, Province of Brandenburg, Kingdom of Prussia, German Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LKing Aleksander II, born Alexander Ferdinand was only eight years old when his family moved to Poland. His youth would be overshadowed by the quite distant and difficult relationship of his parents, and the pressure of expectations: he would have to take up the throne of Poland eventually. Alexander pursued a career in the military, following typical footsteps for a German aristocrat. His service in the Polish Air Force won him much fame and admiration from the Polish public, and in many ways the shared war-time troubles have made him way more accepted in Poland than his father ever was. His decision to marry a Slavic noblewoman instead of a German one also helped to convince the Polish public of their king's dedication to Poland, not to Germany. However, Aleksander is untested in politics, and one can only guess how good a peace-time leader he will be.§!

Kazimierz Zakrzewski
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 4th, 1900, in Kraków, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LPerhaps the leading visionary among the Polish left, Kazimierz Zakrzewski has come a long way from the Krakówian suburbs, to Paris, and back to Poland again. Born in 1900, young Kazimierz started up an academic career in his father's footsteps. His studies were abruptly cut short when the young idealistic-minded student of Antiquity joined the Polish Legions with high hopes of liberating his country from the Tsarist yoke. Like many other Legionnaires, after the war Zakrzewski was disillusioned by the new Kingdom of Poland, which was regarded as little more than a German puppet regime by many. During this time he became interested in the philosophy of socialist thinkers such as Sorel, Jouhaux and Brzozowski, and joined radical \
Leah Feldman
§YBorn:§! §LSeptember 1898, in Odessa, Kherson Governorate, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LLeah Feldman is a truly remarkable woman - she has witnessed Russian, French and British revolutions, fought against the Cossacks with Nestor Makhno, and organised Jewish resistance movements in Palestine. Even her birthplace is not known for sure - she claims Warsaw, while her British passport states Odessa. What is known, however, is that she grew up in a poor Polish-Jewish émigre family in London, and became involved with the local anarchist movement early on. In 1918, she sailed for Petrograd to witness the birth of the first truly socialist nation, but was quickly horrified by the brutality of the Bolsheviks, and decided to join with Nestor Makhno's forces instead - fighting against both the Reds and the Whites until the anarchists were defeated and forced to escape.\n\nFeldman ended up in the young French Commune and joined Lew Marek's group of Polish anarcho-syndicalists. During these years she became truly interested in Poland's cause and was one of the most active members of the Polish Revolutionary Committee after Dr. Zakrzewski, whom she disagreed with in nearly everything. Unlike Zakrzewski, Feldman travelled considerably, joining the British Revolution and later toiled in Palestine for the Jewish freedom. \
Jerzy Czeszejko-Sochacki
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 29th, 1892, in Nieżyn, Chernigov Governorate, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LJerzy Czeszejko-Sochacki studied in Saint Petersburg and was deeply connected to Russian, Lithuanian and Polish communist movements already during the Great War. After the collapse of the Bolsheviks, his party was forced underground - like most of the Polish far-left - and Czeszejko-Sochacki went to exile in France. There he found new ideological foundation from the teaching of Marcel Cachin and the French councilist approach to the revolution. In Poland, Czeszejko-Sochacki has been somewhat outside the power struggle around Zakrzewski.§!
Zygmunt Zaremba
§YBorn:§! §LApril 28th, 1895, in Piotrków, Piotrków Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LZygmunt Zaremba joined the Polish Socialist Party at a very young age, for similar reasons as many others - to further the cause of Polish independence, like his family had done a generation before in the January Uprising. Zaremba was an active student organiser and the youngest member elected to the Central Workers' Committee of the PPS. However, he was not a follower of Piłsudski, nor did he see cooperating with the Germans as a way out. Quite to the contrary, he was adamantly against any kind of coalition talks or cooperation with the monarchy in Poland. His belief in uncompromising workers' democracy seems unflinching, and this attitude has sent him to prison multiple times. Since his latest release, he has been operating from Galicia, crafting connections between Polish and Ukrainian socialist independence activists along with his Ukrainian wife, Natalia. Unlike Kazimierz Zakrzewski and the ZZZ's leadership in France, Zaremba never believed that the Polish liberation could come from any other source than Poles - and their brothers in neighbouring countries.§!
Norbert Barlicki
§YBorn:§! §LJune 6th, 1880, in Sieciechów, Radom Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LNorbert Barlicki rose to national prominence in 1918, when he vehemently opposed the Brest Treaty and read the declaration of free election to the Council of State. For this, he was imprisoned in the Modlin Fortress, and was released only after the war was over. His most radical socialist stances moderated quite a bit afterwards. Instead of the hardline socialist line that many in his party believed in, Barlicki was a man of the united front. Be it idealism or pragmatism, he believed that the best way to help the Polish people was inside the system, through untiring grassroots work to improve people's livelihoods. For this, he fostered close ties with the People's Party, having been affiliated with them already since the PSL's foundation.\n\nThis cooperation finally brought fruit as grand old man of the PSL, Tomasz Nocznicki, became prime minister in the 1930s, with Barlicki a cabinet minister. Many reforms and the widely successful Four-Year Plan were seen as proof that the German-imposed monarchy could serve the common people's interest - and even if the government eventually fell, it gave the current PPS-PSL alliance a very concrete goal.\n\nBarlicki's optimism was unfounded, as after the chaotic 1938 election the Centrolew bloc was swiftly denied forming a government despite their victory. Seeing no other options, Barlicki eventually caved in to the radicals of the bloc, and protests turned into revolution, with the overthrowing of the monarchy and declaration of the republic. In a twist of fate, the only man with faith that the monarchy could have been salvaged, became the republic's first leader. Norbert Barlicki could forge an alliance between the workers and the peasants that overthrew the king, now he needs to keep the front united.§!
Stanisław Wojciechowski
§YBorn:§! §LMarch 15th, 1869, in Kalisz, Kalisz Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LStanisław Wojciechowski was born to a middle-class family in the suburbs of Kalisz, and like many of his compatriots, started his radicalisation during his studies. He participated in the founding congress of the Polish Socialist Party in Paris in 1892, and was an active trade union organiser, often at odds with the police. The French authorities expelled him, and Wojciechowski made his way back to the Kingdom of Poland, where he threw himself into activism. He worked closely with Józef Piłsudski and the PPS, but also maintained contacts to the National Democrats, as he believed that the entire Polish political establishment is needed for independence.\n\nAfter 1905, Wojciechowski started to become disillusioned with political parties and trade unions, and instead focused on agricultural cooperatives as the basis of Polish liberty, and tried to improve the life in the Polish countryside. These efforts were cut short by the Great War. Wojciechowski evacuated to Russia with the National Committee, and tried yet again to forge an alliance with the National Democrats, with little success. Disappointed, he returned to the Kingdom of Poland in 1918, with his family and focused on the social activities and food relief in the war-torn kingdom. In the 1920s, he joined the PSL and became a university lecturer. In his lectures, he always stressed the importance of economic democratisation as a precursor to political democratisation, and held that as long as the kingdom was dominated by capitalists and great landowners, Poland cannot truly be a democracy. A long-time senator, Minister of Agriculture in the Nocznicki cabinet and chairman of the Society of Cooperatives, Wojciechowski is a respected and experienced politician and social activist. While initially reluctant to accept the presidency, he was convinced by interpartisan support from the Sejm to stand for one term - merely to safeguard the young republic's critical first years, and then to withdraw from politics.§!
Jan Woźnicki
§YBorn:§! §LAugust 15th, 1881, in Nieszawa, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LJan Woźnicki is part of the right wing of the PSL. He became a member of the National Democrats in 1904 as part of the struggle against Russification of the education system. He was imprisoned by the Tsarist authorities in 1906 for using his position as teacher to organise resistance, and soon after left the National Democrats, disappointed in Roman Dmowski's peaceful stance and principle of non-violent resistance.\n\nInstead, Woźnicki joined the Polish Legions and soon after, the PSL. He has served in the Sejm multiple times, primarily focusing on education and anti-corruption programmes. The past two decades have made him respected on both sides of the political spectrum as a reliable centrist.§!
Mieczyslaw Michałowicz
§YBorn:§! §LSeptember 10th, 1876, in Saint-Petersburg, Saint-Petersburg Governorate, Russian Empire§!
Stanisław Mikołajczyk
§YBorn:§! §LJuly 18th, 1901, in Dorsten-Holsterhausen, Province of Westphalia, Kingdom of Prussia, German Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LStanisław Mikołajczyk was born into a family of a miners in Prussian countryside. He never progressed into higher education, but that did not stop Mikołajczyk from joining the Prussian section of the People's Party and from organising rural youth movements against Germanisation policies in the Province of Posen. Organising peasant strikes and protests did not bring him support among German authorities, but he did manage to escape any prison sentences. Working together with the Poznanese National Democrats, Mikołajczyk managed to help many Poles buy land and defend their right of ownership against the German Settlement Commission. After annexation of the Province of Posen to Poland, Mikołajczyk was elected into the national Sejm with strong support from his home region. His experiences from the struggles in the Posen Province have convinced him of the need for broad coalitions in Polish politics, as he sees Germans as a greater threat than the National Democrats.§!
Józef Chaciński
§YBorn:§! §LMarch 13th, 1889, in Warsaw, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LJózef Chaciński became involved with the National Democratic youth organisations from an early age, while studying law in Warsaw. After a brief period of imprisonment by the Tsarist authorities, he left the country in 1909 and studied in Switzerland. There he built contacts with the National Democratic movement, and after his return to Poland, joined the Christian-National Party - part of the ChZJN bloc - and became a very visible politician within the bloc, trying to moderate the most radical ideas in the Polish right. Chaciński is a solidarist and believes that both excess liberalism and socialism have failed to increase common people's happiness, but also that the National Democrats should not forget the common peasants.§!
Adam Chądzyński
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 18th, 1882, in Górki-Grubaki, Siedlce Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAdam Chądzyński comes from family of minor landowners, and was initially involved in the National League, but split from them in 1908 over the question of collaboration with the Russians. Chądzyński came to lead the National Worker's Union (NZR), a solidarist and Christian working-class party. The NZR became positioned as a small independent party between MKP and ChZJN, maintaining a small but loyal base. Chądzyński pragmatically supported the Ponikowski governments, but could just as easily side with ChZJN when preferable. This position as an odd centrist between the more right-wing Christian Democrats and the liberal PSL has made Chądzyński much more influential than the mere popularity of his party would suggest.§!
Władysław Grabski
§YBorn:§! §LJuly 7th, 1874, in Borów, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LWładysław Grabski comes from a noble family that made fortunes through sugar beet farming, which secured a good education for Władysław and his siblings, Stanisław and Zofia. Władysław studied agrarian economics, and took over the family estate after his father's death. He believed in the role of the education of the peasantry as a means of strengthening the Polish national identity, and ran an illegal Polish-language school for peasant children. Eventually, he was imprisoned by Tsarist authorities, before becoming a member of the National League, and being elected to the Russian Duma. From there, he focused on improving the quality of life in the Polish countryside, in cooperation with the authorities.\n\nGrabski was imprisoned by the Germans in 1917 after criticising their economic policy in occupied Poland too harshly, but as a silver lining could complete his third volume devoted to the situation of Polish peasantry. Ironically, he went straight from prison to becoming a minister in the Józef Świeżyński's government, thanks to his skill and popularity in Poland. In 1919 he founded the ChZJN with his brother, leading the party to become a major power in the Sejm for over a decade. Even though he stepped aside from leadership positions within the party after the 1920s, he has remained possibly the most popular National Democrat politician in Poland.§!
Wojciech Korfanty
§YBorn:§! §LApril 20th, 1873, in Sadzawki, Province of Silesia, Kingdom of Prussia, German Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LWojciech Korfanty grew up in Silesia, and from early age took part in the struggle for the Polish language and culture against the Prussian authorities. He was expelled from his secondary school after making negative remarks about Otto von Bismarck, but nonetheless graduated in economics and joined the National League. After a brief imprisonment for agitation and leaving Endecja, Korfanty founded his own Democratic-National Society and was elected to the Reichstag, with even some German support. His deeply held Christian Democratic convictions made him relatively popular among Catholics across the language barrier, and it made him somewhat of a centrist candidate after Upper Silesia's integration into Republic of Poland, as he is Christian Democrat but not adherent of Roman Dmowski.§!
Edward Rydz-Śmigły

§YBorn:§! §LMarch 11th, 1886, in Brzeżany, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAs a son of an impoverished family from Eastern Galicia, who lost both of his parents before his tenth birthday, young Edward's life did not seem to offer much. His foster family was wealthy enough to offer Rydz, a promising painter and artist, a chance to get an education. In the Jagiellonian University Rydz joined the Union of Active Combat and Riflemen's Association and became an adherent of Józef Piłsudski. Adopting the pseudonym \
Roman Dmowski
§YBorn:§! §LAugust 9th, 1865, in Kamionek, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!
Adam Doboszyński
§YBorn:§! §LJanuary 11th, 1904, in Kraków, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAdam Doboszyński was born into a family of landed gentry in Kraków. His father was a lawyer and a liberal politician. At first, it did not seem obvious that he would become one of the most prolific Polish far-right theorists - his youth was relatively normal, and he was only a child during the Great War. After his father's death in 1929, Adam took over the family estate near Kraków. During this time, he started writing.\n\nThe Creditanstalt Crisis in 1931 shook the whole Austro-Hungarian realm dramatically, and inspired Doboszyński to associate with the National Democrat movement in both Poland and Galicia, becoming increasingly dissatisfied with the state of both Polands. Doboszyński travelled widely and became greatly inspired by theorists of distributism, such as Gilbert Chesterton, and the larger movement of Catholic social teachings. Doboszyński became a political theorist in his own right, and started to merge Polish nationalism with the social science of the Catholic Church. Using the relatively liberal publishing laws of Galicia to become a prolific author, his texts were distributed widely on both sides of the border. Doboszyński criticised both capitalism and socialism as structures that crush the human spirit, and called for a spiritual awakening of the Poles. Many say that he correctly predicted the German economic crash of 1936.\n\nBy 1936, Doboszyński had become a National Democrat organiser and speaker, touring the Polish countryside and agitating against the foreign monarchs ruling over Poles, against the Freemasons and Jews who controlled the Polish society, against the socialists and liberals who did not understand the good of Poland. Despite multiple close calls with the police and periods of underground activity, Doboszyński's fame only grew, and reached its pinnacle when he led a raid into Myślenice and stuck against the town's Jewish population with his militia. After a minimal time in prison, he fled to Russia, to the National League's exiled leadership, and became the leader of the National Democrat movement after Dmowski's death.\n\nIn power, Doboszyński is finally in a position to put his theories into practice. In his vision, the ideal society is that of the pre-Enlightenment era, where industrialisation had not yet released man's greed and lust, and where a strong family unit and the Church provided meaningful and moral social surroundings for people. It is his belief that with tireless work, this bygone golden age of humanity can be brought back.§!
Wolfgang von Kries
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 27th, 1868, in Schmentau, Province of Prussia, Kingdom of Prussia, North German Confederation§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBorn in rural Pomerania to a Thuringian family, von Kries aimed for an administrative career. He served in the army, as was expected from a Prussian nobleman, and was appointed into the administration of the Generalgouvernement Warschau during the Weltkrieg. He proved to be a natural-born organiser, with an eye for detail and great ability to learn everything there was to know about his new duties. These qualities secured him promotions, and by the end of the war he was the Governor-General of Poland. With this role he saw through many economic reforms in Poland, such as the creation of the Polish Mark. After the coronation of August IV, von Kries, who knew the Polish situation possibly better than any other German, was kept as the king's right-hand man and eventually stationed to Poland permanently as a military attaché and head of the German military mission in Poland.§!
Eustachy Sapieha
§YBorn:§! §LAugust 2nd, 1881, in Biłka Szlachecka, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LEustachy Sapieha comes from an old princely family of szlachta, rich and well-known. His political career began in 1916, when he became a relief organiser in Warsaw and an associate of Józef Piłsudski. He became one of the earliest diplomats in the service of the Kingdom of Poland, and was sent to Switzerland to negotiate with the Polish National Committee. Despite Roman Dmowski not being willing to cooperate, many centrist National Democrats joined the Interpartisan Circle from Sapieha's encouragement. Eustachy Sapieha would serve as the Polish ambassador in Austria in the 1920s and became part of King August IV's inner circle. Politically, he is seen as a liberal-conservative centrist and is known as an opportunist politician and tenacious negotiator.§!
Antoni Ponikowski
§YBorn:§! §LMay 29th, 1878, in Siedlce, Siedlce Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAntoni Ponikowski has become something of the king's trusted man. Initially a member of the National Democrats and later the Christian Democrats, Ponikowski was among the Polish intellectuals during the Great War who turned towards building the Kingdom of Poland, rather than continue Roman Dmowski's hopeless war. Ponikowski became one of the leading figures of the Interpartisan Political Circle, formed out of the conservatives and moderate liberals who supported the Regency.\n\nPonikowski has served multiple times as the prime minister. During his terms, Ponikowski has sought to walk the narrow middle path, cooperating both with moderate liberals and Christian Democrats. Ponikowski has been largely untainted by political scandals and is a commonly a patron for Polish composers. At the latter stages of his political career, his retirement had already been planned, but the immensely close 1934 election could not allow anyone else to form a cabinet.§!
Aleksy Ćwiakowski
§YBorn:§! §LApril 5th, 1895, in Sygontka, Piotrków Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAleksy Ćwiakowski was born into a peasant family near Częstochowa and studied law in the Jagiellonian University. He was briefly active in the Polish Military Organisation, but abandoned activism quickly and joined the PSL. Disappointed in the party's anti-monarchist stance, he founded his own party, the Progressive Peasant Union, which eventually merged into the MKP in 1934.\n\nĆwiakowski came to represent the young wing of the monarchists, and focused his efforts in winning over the peasantry with policies competing with the PSL's ones. As a surprise decision from the king, Ćwiakowski was appointed as the prime minister after the 1938 election as someone new and a visionary who could reform the monarchy into something with lasting popular support.§!
Władysław Studnicki
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 15th, 1867, in Dyneburg, Vitebsk Governorate, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LWładysław Studnicki was a socialist radical in his youth, and was sent to Siberia for eight years. After his exile, he moved to Vienna and shifted rightwards in his political beliefs. However, he did not embrace National Democrat passivism - rather, he argued that an independent Poland could only be achieved through an alliance with the Central Powers and the dismemberment of Russia. He briefly flirted with the idea of an Austro-Polish union, but after the Dual Monarchy's failures in the Great War, Studnicki became convinced of a pro-German orientation being the only sensible option.\n\nHis rise to national prominence started in 1916, when he was deeply involved in founding the Kingdom of Poland, and was among the chief negotiators for the treaty that secured Chełm for Poland. His political organisation, The Polish Statehood Party, grew into a large part of the MKP with support from Polish Germans, industrialists and other pro-German conservatives. In Studnicki's beliefs, democracy has no special value compared to the continuing statehood of Poland - which should ultimately be the only thing that matters.§!
Stanisław Dubois
§YBorn:§! §LJanuary 9th, 1901, in Warsaw, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LStanisław Dubois is the great-grandson of an officer of the Napoleonic army, and the son of a lawyer. His political awakening came in 1918, when Norbert Barlicki held a speech demanding free elections in the Kingdom of Poland and condemning the Treaty of Brest. Already a radical student, in Barlicki's tutorship Dubois adopted radically socialist views - though unlike his mentor, Dubois hardly moderated with age. He became an organiser and activist in the PPS, and was appointed as an editor of Poland's most popular political magazine, the Robotnik, in the late 1920s. During his editorship, the Robotnik was often censored for its uncompromising critique towards the monarchy's elites.\n\nWhile his mentor Barlicki became the leader of the party's moderates, Dubois radicalised in the other direction, continuously pushing for a more uncompromising stance, building contacts with the far-left and radical republicans. During the 1938 revolution, Dubois was the leading figure in organising the general strike, and became de-facto leader of the uprising in Warsaw. Now appointed as prime minister, Dubois is finally in a position to ensure that Poland never again slips into authoritarianism.§!
Maksymilian Malinowski

He became a publisher of peasant activist papers and eventually moved to Warsaw after he was no longer welcome in Prussia. There he joined the National League in 1900, though he was quickly disappointed in the passivist stance of Roman Dmowski.\n\nMalinowski started to sketch an ideology of his own, in his magazine \
Bogusław Miedziński
§YBorn:§! §LMarch 22nd, 1891, in Miastków Kościelny, Siedlce Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBogusław Miedziński became involved with the PPS Combat Organisation already when he was 15 years old, and dedicated his early years to the Polish Legions. As an able organiser, Miedziński became adjutant and second-in-command to Edward Rydz-Śmigły in the Polish Military Organisation. It was during these years when he developed unflinching loyalty towards Rydz, and became part of his small group of trusted companions. After the ceasefire, the men stayed in contact, even if they were forced to the side-lines of the political life in the kingdom. Miedziński was elected to the Sejm from the PSL's list, and quickly gained the reputation of a political opportunist and backstabber, and an opponent to the monarchy, loyal only to other ex-Legionaries. He envisioned the ideal Republic of Poland to be guided by the military, likening it to the rule of the Shogun in medieval Japan - and this military leader would naturally be Rydz-Śmigły.§!

Paweł Lew Marek
§YBorn:§! §LAugust 16th, 1901, in Radymno, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LPaweł Marek was a Galician Jew initially active in Lwów, where he tried to forge cooperation between Polish, Jewish and Ukrainian anarcho-syndicalists. After having multiple clashes with the authorities, he emigrated to France, where he joined the National Revolutionary Committee of Polish socialist exiles. In 1931, he was sent to Poland to coordinate syndicalist activities there, and under his leadership the Anarchist Federation of Poland grew into a formidable organisation, inspired by the dream of democratic syndicalism and a Poland free of ethnic persecution and political oppression.§!

Maurycy Klemens Zamoyski
§YBorn:§! §LJune 30th, 1871, in Warsaw, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LCount Maurycy Zamoyski comes from a wealthy aristocratic family with significant estates in both Galicia and Poland proper. Zamoyski had already joined the National League by 1905, and became a proponent of cooperation between the Slavic brothers of Poland and Russia. Despite his pro-Entente sympathies, and even his role as the vice-president of the Polish National Committee in 1918, he returned to Poland in 1919 and was elected to the Senate in the first elections. Zamoyski, possibly the richest man in Poland, maintained a cautious stance towards the monarchy and was among the top patrons of ChZJN. However, he has preferred to appear as a moderate in politics, and not tie himself too tightly into any single political party.§!
Stanisław Downarowicz
§YBorn:§! §LMarch 28th, 1874, in Łochów, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LStanisław Downarowicz was a strangely centrist independence activist. Despite his initial cooperation with the PPS, which led to his imprisonment by Tsarist authorities, he acted as a Rifle Team organiser without following either Piłsudski or Dmowski. During this time he was in exile in Galicia, building many contacts in Lwów, until he could return to the Kingdom. He joined the Polish Progressive Party in 1910, which would later become one of the core parties inside the MKP, and has kept to a moderate liberal political path ever since. Downarowicz is among the most visible faces among MKP's progressives, who seek to liberalise the kingdom, rather than go ever-deeper into authoritarianism.§!
Janusz Radziwiłł
§YBorn:§! §LSeptember 3rd, 1880, in Berlin, Province of Brandenburg, Kingdom of Prussia, German Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LPrince Janusz Radziwiłł, often titled the man behind the king, is a wealthy aristocrat and magnate, whose palace in Warsaw has become the unofficial meeting place of the Polish landowning and corporate elite. He has been a supporter of the monarchy from its very first days, and was the Regency Council's ambassador to the negotiations concerning the appointment of the Polish king. Some say that he should have insisted on an Austrian monarch; others applaud him for his negotiation skills that secured Chełm for Poland. Ever since, he has been in the heart of August IV's circle of advisors, carefully balancing between supporting the king, and acting as an independent-minded conservative, able to draw allies from across all political parties.§!

Konstanty Rokossowski
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 21st, 1896, in Warsaw, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBorn to a mixed Polish-Russian family, Konstanty Rokossowski hails from the noble Rokossowski family, even though his father, a railway officer, was as removed from traditional nobility as one could get. Rokossowski had a difficult childhood: he lost his father early in his life and he had to abandon school in order to make a living for the rest of his family. He caught up by reading books in Russian and Polish in his spare time, only to fuel his life-long conflict on whether he was Russian, Polish, or something in between.\n\nIn 1914, alongside his cousin Franz, the 18-year old Konstanty signed up for the retreating Russian armies and became a cavalryman, serving with distinction and achieving the rank of non-commissioned officer. They had to part ways when the October Revolution broke out however - Franz joined the Polish Military Organisation, and Konstanty was one of the first to volunteer for the Red Army. There, he quickly rose in rank as a capable cavalry commander but he could not turn the tide - the Soviet regime collapsed and fell, and Rokossowski was forced to flee.\n\nAfter years of soul-searching across Europe, he ended up in France, where he came into contact with the Polish socialists in exile and enrolled in the French army officer corps. Though he maintains contact with the other former Bolsheviks, Rokossowski has grown distant from the Russian revolutionary cause and thus did not follow them to Argentina - his eyes turn towards Poland instead. Perhaps there, in his other homeland, he can find purpose.§!
Kazimierz Sosnkowski
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 19th, 1885, in Warsaw, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LKazimierz Sosnkowski, descended from poor nobility on his mother's side, was a passionate composer and interested in architecture in his youth. His academic career was cut short by the revolutionary mood in Poland, which prompted him to join the Polish Socialist Party and their struggle against the Tsarist officials, because he saw it as the only organisation that was willing to truly fight for an independent Poland. He was forced to move to Kraków to avoid the Russian police, and there he met Józef Piłsudski, a man he would idolise like so many other to-be-Legionnaires. He continued the fight in the PPS Combat Organisation and was a co-founder of the Riflemen's Association, finishing his studies of architecture in the meantime.\n\nDuring the Weltkrieg, Sosnkowski became Piłsudski's right-hand-man and proved to be a brilliant and cool-headed field commander. He was arrested alongside Piłsudski and Slawek during the Oath Crisis and spent the remainder of the war in prison. During his imprisonment he played over 500 games of chess with Piłsudski, developing a passion for the game. After the war he abandoned his political activism for some time, focusing on music instead.\n\nSosnkowski was allowed to return to the army in the late 1920s, but was kept to the sidelines due to his close friendship with Piłsudski. Too skilled to be kept in menial duties for long, eventually he served in multiple military delegations and other organisational duties, speaking fluent German, French, English, Italian, Russian and Greek.§!\n\n§YNotes: Suspected to harbour sympathies for the republicans, but is officially apolitical.§!

Both his father and grandfather took part in the failed Polish uprisings, and he was raised in deeply patriotic and Catholic surroundings. Stanisław Sosabowski
§YBorn:§! §LMay 8th, 1892, in Stanisławów, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LStanisław Sosabowski was born to a poor working-class family in Stanisławów. During his meagre studies he became involved with activist, pioneering, and scouting organisations, before being drafted into the Austro-Hungarian army in 1913. His baptism of fire was in the great Przemyśl fortress. Sosabowski took part in mountain fighting in Slovakia and was grievously wounded multiple times, receiving all medals available for non-commissioned officers before the end of the war.\n\nAt his own request, Sosabowski was transferred to Lublin and took part in organising the Army of the Kingdom of Poland after being promoted to lieutenant. He continued his studies in the Warsaw Military Academy after the war, and focused on the training of the Polish special forces and later on even took part in German Fallschmirjäger exercises, planning to implement the usage of paratroopers into the Polish special service.§!
Adam Koc
§YBorn:§! §LJuly 31st, 1891, in Suwałki, Suwałki Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAdam Koc was born into an aristocratic family from Podlasie. His career in independence activism began during the 1905 revolution as he took part in a school strike - an action that led to Koc being expelled from his school. He joined the National Workers' Union, a pro-Endecja party, and radicalised further during his studies in Kraków. Even though he considered Władysław Studnicki his mentor, Koc nonetheless joined the Union of Active Struggle despite its socialist leanings. He met Józef Piłsudski and the other famous Legionnaires and eventually became Slawek's deputy in the Polish Military Organisation, organising partisan actions behind the lines in the Russian part of Poland.\n\nAs the war continued, Koc wanted desperately to get to the frontline. As it was impossible to cross no-mans-land, he travelled illegally through Finland, and after multiple unexpected problems he made his way to Piłsudski's headquarters and officially joined the Legion. He ended up participating in the occupation of Lublin, but after getting caught for taking part in nationalist agitation among the Lublin Poles, he was sent to the frontline despite his bad eyesight. There, he was badly wounded, but kept organising support for Piłsudski in hospital, and later, in imprisonment.\n\nAfter his release in 1919, Koc befriended Edward Rydz-Śmigły during their brief exile in Galicia before being allowed to return to their homeland. He was elected to the Sejm as a nonpartisan delegate, though he supported the Christian-Democratic Party in many of their initiatives. Also active in many veterans' organisations, Koc became an important figure among the Polish right-wing, often being seen as Rydz-Śmigły's right-hand man.§!\n\n§YNotes: Associate of Edward Rydz-Śmigły.§!

Józef Dowbor-Muśnicki
§YBorn:§! §LOctober 25th, 1867, in Garbów, Lublin Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LJózef Dowbor-Muśnicki's roots can be traced to a Calvinist Lithuanian family who settled Sandomierz in the 17th century. He served thirty years in the Russian Army, seeing action in the Far East and in the Great War. By 1917 he was a Lieutenant General and appointed as the commander-in-chief of the Polish forces in Russia, with the goal of organising the Polish soldiers into their own national units. The Polish Corps in Russia had roughly 64,000 men at their command, making Dowbor-Muśnicki an important warlord in the constantly shifting Russian political situation, where initially the Poles cooperated with Aleksandr Kerensky's government, since it had promised autonomy for Russian minorities.\n\nWhen the October Revolution brought the Bolsheviks to power, Dowbor-Muśnicki tried to tread the path of neutrality, but was thrown into the White side after the Poles resisted all attempts to disarm them. His corps took control of the city of Bobruisk in White Ruthenia, and held against the Bolsheviks until the Brest-Litovsk Treaty was signed and the Bolsheviks retreated from the western governorates. The German army surrounded Bobruisk, and a standoff ensued. Many of Dowbor-Muśnicki's officers sought to fight the Germans to the last, and tried to pull off an unsuccessful coup against the esteemed general. After their failure, Dowbor-Muśnicki surrendered, and was transferred to the newly-established Kingdom of Poland as a prisoner of war alongside his army.\n\nAfter the war, Dowbor-Muśnicki entered the service of the new Polish Army, and became one of its most experienced generals. He has done much to ensure the army's apolitical nature - both by keeping the radicals from promotions, but also by turning a blind eye to old events. In Dowbor-Muśnicki's words, he is a nationalist and only a nationalist, believing that the military men should not hold political opinions. This attitude has made him seem very supportive towards the status quo of the German-backed monarchy, and his enemies in the political extremes like to paint Dowbor-Muśnicki as a coward who has only served foreign masters all his life. Zbigniew Antonowicz
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 14th, 1894, in Kalisz, Kalisz Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LZbigniew Antonowicz's military career began in 1915, when he volunteered for the Puławy Legion, which was created as a Russian counterpart to the Polish Legions fighting with the Central Powers. Antonowicz lost an eye when the Legion was covering the Russian retreat over the Nurcem River. Despite his wounds, he managed to bring reinforcement orders to his unit HQ. After a year of recovering, he continued the war regardless. The Legion was later disbanded, and in 1917 he joined Józef Dowbor-Muśnicki's 1st Polish Corps. Alongside the unit, he was eventually integrated into the Polish Army. As an able engineer, Antonowicz's military career has been mostly focused on fortification works.§!
Mikołaj Bołtuć
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 20th, 1893, in Saint-Petersburg, Saint-Petersburg Governorate, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LMikołaj Bołtuć graduated from the Pavlovsk Infantry School in 1914 and was sent straight into the firing line. He fought on the Baltic Front and in 1917 as part of the Polish Legion in the Finnish Civil War, which fought against the Finnish Reds mostly on the Karelian Front. After the collapse of the Tsarist regime, Bołtuć joined the 3rd Polish Corps in Russia, mostly operating in Ukraine. In June 1918 the unit capitulated to Austrian forces, and among others Bołtuć was sent to Poland after agreeing to serve in the new royal army. Bołtuć is seen as a dependable and careful leader, and is a personally firm believer of an apolitical and nonreligious military.§!
Władysław Anders
§YBorn:§! §LAugust 11th, 1892, in Błonie, Warsaw Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LWładysław Anders is from an old Reformed Livonian German family. He entered the Russian army in 1910 as a volunteer and sought to serve in the dragoons, being an ardent lover of horse riding. He fought on the Romanian Front, and was wounded multiple time during the Weltkrieg. After the collapse of the Russian Army, Anders joined Józef Dowbor-Muśnicki's 1st Polish Corps in Russia and fought alongside them until the corps surrendered to the Germans at Bobruisk and was eventually integrated into the Polish Army. Like Dowbor-Muśnicki, Anders is staunchly apolitical.\n\nAnders is quite a famous equestrian, and is also an active member of the Military Automobile and Motorcycle Club. To him, cavalry is a rapid weapon, and at a time where cavalry is losing their ability compared to modern weapons, something else must take their place as a rapid, mobile force capable of flanking manoeuvres. This mindset, and his brilliant combat record, brought him the command of the first Polish Armoured Division, formed as part of Heinz Guderian's reforms.§!
Henryk Dobrzanski
§YBorn:§! §LJune 22nd, 1897, in Jasło, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LHenryk Dobrzański was barely 18 when he abandoned his studies, lied about his age and joined the Polish Legions, where he was a reckless cavalryman and earned a rapid promotion. During the Oath Crisis, he was in the minority of the Legionaries who accepted to swear allegiance to the Kaiser. He proved his loyalty yet again in Rarańcza, where his superior, Józef Haller, ordered the unit to turn against their Austrian allies and escape to the Russian side. Dobrzański and men close to him did not obey, and were imprisoned in a Hungarian prison camp for the mutiny of their comrades. After the war, he returned to the Polish service, and showed a good understanding of manoeuvre warfare and hit-and-run tactics. In civilian life he is a famous horse rider, who has brought Poland international fame in winning numerous prestigious races and competitions.§!
Juliusz Rómmel
§YBorn:§! §LJune 3rd, 1881, in Grodno, Grodno Governorate, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LJuliusz Rómmel comes from the old, polonised Baltic German family of von Rummel. He followed his family's military tradition and joined the Russian Army, rising rapidly through the ranks during the Russo-Japanese War and on the Austrian Front in the Great War. He led 3rd Polish Corps in Kyïv in 1917, when he surrendered with his men to the Austrian Army, and was swiftly integrated into the new Polish Army. In the last war, he was proved especially skilled in manoeuvre warfare on the wide plains of Ukraine, utilising cavalry and light artillery effectively - though his opponents often paint him as the very example of anachronism who still believes in the strategies of 1917 to fight his wars.§!
Wacław Stachiewicz
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 19th, 1894, in Lwów, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LWacław Stachiewicz was always more loyal to Edward Rydz-Śmigły than to Józef Piłsudski, having acted as Rydz's close associate already in the Polish Legions. Stachiewicz is an intelligent and well-educated soldier with deep interests in military topology and cartography. As an able officer, he was accepted to the newly-founded Polish army and has primarily been in organisational roles, though his career advance has been slow, as it's been hampered by his close association with nationalist circles.§!\n\n§YNotes: Associate of Edward Rydz-Śmigły.§!
Stanisław Magnuszewski
§YBorn:§! §LSeptember 17th, 1888, in Dyneburg, Vitebsk Governorate, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LStanisław Magnuszewski was born in Latvia and from an early age aimed for a military career, graduating from the Alexeyev Military School in Moscow as part of the military engineering corps. Magnuszewski was primarily an organiser and an engineer, rather than a very bold soldier. He rose steadily and eventually served under General Józef Dowbor Muśnicki's corps, and was involved in the dramatic capture of Bobruisk fortress by the Polish Corps. Like the entire unit, he was demobilised and integrated into the Polnische Wehrmacht. During peacetime, he has served as a military teacher.§!
Antoni Szylling
§YBorn:§! §LAugust 31st, 1884, in Płoniawy, Łomża Governorate, Kingdom of Poland, Russian Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAntoni Szylling was forced into a military career after multiple clashes with Tsarist authorities threatened prolonged imprisonment. He was a member of a secret socialist combat organisation, owned numerous illegal weapons, and after a brief arrest, he was drafted into Russian army in lieu of continuing his sentence. Despite this rocky start, he proved to be a decent officer, and was a Major by the time Russia exited the war. After being taken prisoner, Szylling agreed to serve in the new Polish army. He is best described as a cautious, bland tactician, who avoids risks and fulfils orders to the word. Such attitude has served him well in the army, leading to several promotions.§!
Bolesław Wieniawa-Długoszowski
§YBorn:§! §LJuly 22nd, 1881, in Maksymówka, Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria, Austria-Hungary§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBolesław Wieniawa-Długoszowski is a colourful officer. Doctor, poet, a man whose two passions are women and horses. In his youth, the young scion of the prestigious Wieniawa family would rather associating with the bohemian art scene, than focusing on his studies. His serial relationships and conversion to Protestantism already made him quite infamous in the high echelons of Polish society. In contrast, his military career began similarly to many others'. He joined the Riflemen's Association and followed Józef Piłsudski through the trenches of Eastern Europe as his personal adjutant, where he was decorated with the highest awards for bravery for his daring cavalry operations.\n\nWieniawa-Długoszowski spent some time imprisoned alongside Piłsudski, but was allowed to continue serving in the newly-established Polish Army. He was passionate about cavalry warfare, and acted as trainer and inspector whenever he was not posted abroad as military attaché, or in some kind of trouble - such as being sentenced to five days in prison for duelling. Art remained his true passion, and he arguably spent more time translating literature into Polish, and writing his own works, than he spent in military exercises.§!
Józef Unrug
§YBorn:§! §LOctober 7th, 1884, in Brandenburg an der Havel, Province of Brandenburg, Kingdom of Prussia, German Empire§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBorn Joseph von Unruh, Unrug was raised as part of the wealthy Prussian aristocracy with a long military tradition. He graduated from the naval college in 1907 and commanded a U-boat in the Weltkrieg. Von Unruh was already before the war disenchanted with modern Germany, and developed a strange form of polonophilia, believing that the Poles had the romanticism and the passion that was lacking in Germany. This was supported by him being partially of Polish descent from his father's side. He disagreed with Germany's new Mahanist doctrine of a blue water navy. As a romanticist disappointed with the Kaiserliche Marine, he was mostly kept in training duties in the Baltic. After the war, he has served in Germany's Baltic Fleet before the tumultuous events of the Second Weltkrieg and the re-establishment of the Polish navy after two centuries. Now Józef Unrug, the admiral has been able to combine two of his passions: sailing and Polish culture.§!

Effeminate King
§LThe King is noted for his flamboyant jewellery and for keeping the company of artists rather than officers. It does not make him hugely popular with the Poles, but it makes him even less popular with his father, the Kaiser.§!
The Geheimrat
